Week 10: Querying the History database of Chrome¶

Author: Eni Mustafaraj
Date: Nov 5, 2017

OVERVIEW: Today we will perform two steps in the data science cycle, getting data and exploring data. These are iterative steps, as we know. We might need to be in this loop for a while, until we get to know better what questions we can answer with this data.

Table of Contents

  1. Accessing the History page
  2. The sqlite3 module
  3. Explore the tables in the database
  4. Explore visits
  5. Find most visited websites
  6. Connecting with pandas
  7. Tasks for you

More information

We're disussing the Chrome history file in this notebook. If you want to see screenshots of what the tables in the database look like, you can visit this page.

Additionally, it is possible to download an app that has a GUI interface to inspect the database.
This browser is recommended by our TA Clara: SQLite browser.

1. Accessing the History page¶

The Chrome browser keeps track of interactions you perform on the Chrome browser:

  • searches you perform on the "location" bar
  • links you open either directly or by clicking on links on a page

All this information is stored in a file named 'History'. In the Mac computer, you can access
this file by going to this local URL (after replacing USERNAME with your own):

file:///Users/USERNAME/Library/Application%20Support/Google/Chrome/Default/

On Windows, you should be able to do the same with the path:

C:\Users\USERNAME\AppData\Local\Google\Chrome\User Data\Default

If you download this file from this page in the browser, you can move it to the folder
that contains this notebook as well. Alternatively, once you make sure that it exists
and contains enough content, you can use the path to it instead.

If you open the file, at the very top you'll notice: SQLite format 3. That tells us that
this file is a database file that we can access in Python via the sqlite3 module.

2. The sqlite3 module¶

The most common way to store data is as tables in databases. There are many database systems out there, with the most used ones being relational database management systems, which make use of SQL (structured query language). However, NoSQL databases systems like MongoDB or Redis are also very popular.

SQLite is a database management system that doesn't require a server, which makes it different from more traditional systems like MySQL or Oracle.

To learn more about SQLite, I recommend their tutorial. Since we will not be creating our own databases in this class, we don't need to learn much about it. However, taking the CS 304 Databases course at some point is a good investment.

We will use Python's interface sqlite3 to talk to an SQLite file in order to read the content of it and submit different queries.

There is a very short (one page) tutorial in their website that shows how to talk to SQLite only to query data.

In [81]:
import sqlite3

To talk to a database, we need to establish a connection to it. It's a bit similar to opening files, we have to provide the path to the database.

In [82]:
 path = r"C:\Users\vikiz\Desktop\Week10-ChromeHistory\History" # if you stored the file in the folder

# alternatively, we can use the path to the real History file (not a copy of it)
# remember to change the USERNAME
#path = "C:\Users\vikiz\Desktop\Week10-ChromeHistory"
In [83]:
conn = sqlite3.connect(path)
conn
Out[83]:
<sqlite3.Connection at 0x907fbf8>

To talk to the database now that we have a connection, we need to create a "cursor" object.
Through the cursor we will execute queries in the database, for example, find the tables
of a database:

In [84]:
cursor = conn.cursor()
cursor
# cursor.execute("SELECT name FROM sqlite_master WHERE type='table';")
# for data in cursor.fetchall():
#     print data
Out[84]:
<sqlite3.Cursor at 0x95570a0>

We saw above an example of a query: "SELECT name FROM sqlite_master WHERE type='table';". This is a bit more complicated that the queries we will be usually running, because we didn't know what tables are in the database. Now that we know the names of the tables, we can write queries to read rows from these tables.

In [85]:
# This means: select everything from the table urls
cursor = conn.execute('select * from urls')

# This asks to show a description of the columns of the table
cursor.description
Out[85]:

for i in range(10):
    print rows[i]

Out[94]:

Indeed, it's Wikipedia. I'm going to look up all my sources of queries.
NOTE: The example below contains two different ways to provide a value to a query. It turns out, the query expects a tuple. So, if a value is not already a tuple, we need to represent it as one, for example, (keyID,).

In [95]:
for keyID in sorted(keywordCounter):
    cursor = conn.execute('select url_id from keyword_search_terms where keyword_id = ?', (keyID,))
    urlID = cursor.fetchall()[0] # get the URL id
    # print urlID (it's a tuple, exactly what needed for the query below)
    cursor = conn.execute('select * from urls where id = ?', urlID)
    print "id={}, count={},".format(keyID, keywordCounter[keyID]),
    url = cursor.fetchall()[0][1]
    print url

There are a few interesting observations to make here:

  1. The segment_id seems like the value that links together several consequtive visits.
  2. Apparently Chrome calculates the visit duration, that's good to have. Though the numbers are in microseconds.
  3. The visit_time is also in a strange format: number of microseconds since January, 1601. See this SO stack.

Let's look at some segments:

ATTENTION: Choose a segment_id that appears in your data, the value 191 might not be in yours.

In [99]:
cursor = conn.execute('select * from visits where segment_id == 191')
cursor.fetchall()[:10]
Out[99]:
[]

To make sense of these data, let's do two things:

  • create meaningful timestamps
  • connect to the URLs

Figuring out the time stamps¶

This SO thread explains how to convert Chrome timestamps into datetime:

In [126]:
import datetime

def convertChromeTime(ms):
    """Convert the amount of microsends into a datetime object. 
    Google chrome doesn't use Unix epoch.
    """
    return datetime.datetime(1601, 1, 1) + datetime.timedelta(microseconds=ms)

Let's test the function:

In [127]:
str(convertChromeTime(13146779317202318))
Out[127]:
'2017-08-09 19:08:37.202318'

Let's print now both the datetime and the URL of each visit:

ATTENTION: You might need another id instead of 191.

In [128]:
cursor = conn.execute('select * from visits where segment_id == 191')
for row in cursor.fetchall()[:10]:
    date = convertChromeTime(row[2]) # index of date
    url = conn.execute('select url from urls where id = ?', (row[1],)).fetchall()[0]
    print str(date)[:19], url

But this data contains a lot of repetitions, because it's looking at unique URLs, instead of domain names. Thus, it makes sense to only look for domain names.

Extracting domain names from URLs¶

We don't need to do anything special, we simply use one of Python's libraries for parsing URLs:

In [104]:
from urllib2 import urlparse
In [105]:
urlparse.urlparse('https://portal.wellesley.edu/frontpage?destination=frontpage')
Out[105]:
ParseResult(scheme='https', netloc='portal.wellesley.edu', path='/frontpage', params='', query='destination=frontpage', fragment='')

There are multiple parts here, we are interested in "netloc":

In [106]:
urlparse.urlparse('https://portal.wellesley.edu/frontpage?destination=frontpage').netloc
Out[106]:
'portal.wellesley.edu'

Counting domain names¶

The algorithm:

  • get every URL and its count as it is shown in the urls table
  • extract the domain name (that leaves out specific parts of the URL)
  • update count of domain names
In [171]:
from collections import Counter

domainCounter = Counter()
cursor = conn.execute('select url, visit_count from urls')

for row in cursor.fetchall():
    url, count = row # grab the exisiting count for that URL
    print url
    domain = urlparse.urlparse(url).netloc
    domainCounter[domain] += count # accumulate URL counts
    
domainCounter.most_common(10)

Joining two tables¶

As we see in the tables above, the URL name is only in the urls table, while the two other tables contain an ID. This means that we have to look up the URL using the ID. A better way is to combine the two tables together, that is, to join them.

Joining is a topic in database theory, you can read more in this website: SQLite inner join.

For our two tables visits and urls, we'll do the following

SELECT urls.url, visit_time, visit_duration 
FROM visits
INNER JOIN urls on urls.id = visits.url

let's try it out:

In [117]:
cursor = conn.execute("SELECT urls.url, visit_time, visit_duration FROM visits INNER JOIN urls on urls.id = visits.url")
cursor.fetchall()[:3]
Out[117]:
[(u'data:text/html,chromewebdata', 13146889525207104L, 0),

In [118]:
query = "SELECT urls.url, visit_time, visit_duration FROM visits INNER JOIN urls on urls.id = visits.url"
visitAndURLs = pd.read_sql_query(query, conn)

7. Tasks for you¶

Now that the data is in pandas or you learned how to get it into pandas, it's a matter of dealing with pandas to do the kind of exploratory analysis you need.

Here are some questions to explore:

  1. create a timeseries (add a datetime index as we learned in the Time Series notebook) and do resampling by day to find days with the highest activity. Create plots to see if there are any patterns.
  2. search for certain websites to explore your behavior with respect to them, how often do you visit them, how much time you spend on them

ADVANCED TASKS (students who are willing to go the extra mile)

  1. explore the 'visit_duration' field, does it make sense, or is counting the time bewteen two visits to an open tab?
  2. explore the other tables in the database, is it possible to get sequences that show us how a search session on Google happens?

Task 1¶

In [166]:
from dateutil import parser 
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
df = visitAndURLs
dates = []
list(df['visit_time'])

for date in df['visit_time']:
    dates.append(str(date))
    #print type(date)
v = pd.Series(dates)
visitTimeSeries = pd.Series(1, index= v.apply(parser.parse), name = 'timeseries')
#visitTimeSeries = pd.to_datetime(visitTimeSeries.index)
visitTimeSeries
resample = visitTimeSeries.resample("D",how = "sum")
resample.plot()
plt.show()

the new syntax is .resample(...).sum()
